class="wrap" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a (1536\xe2\x80\x931691)">Early modern Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Isle_of_Man#Early_Modern_period" title="History of the Isle of Man">Early modern Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="wrap" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left"><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_Isles#19th_century" title="History of the British Isles">Late modern period</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;text-align:left">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (since 1707)\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="History of the United Kingdom during the First World War">First World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x931945" title="History of the United Kingdom">Second World War</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (political history)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Post-war period (social history)</a></li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="History of the Republic of Ireland">Late modern Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Isle_of_Man#Modern_period" title="History of the Isle of Man">Late modern Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The first evidence of human presence in Ireland dates to about 12,000 years ago, shortly after the receding of the ice after the <a href="/wiki/Younger_Dryas" title="Younger Dryas">younger Dryas</a> cold phase of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary</a> ended around 9700 BCE, and heralds the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Ireland" title="Prehistoric Ireland">Prehistoric Ireland</a>, which includes the archaeological periods known as the  <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> from about 4000 BCE, the Copper and Bronze Age from about 2300 BCE and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> beginning about 600 BCE. Ireland\'s prehistory ends with the emergence of "<a href="/wiki/Protohistory_of_Ireland" title="Protohistory of Ireland">protohistoric</a>" <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Ireland</a> in the 2nd and 3rd centuries BCE.\n</p><p>By the late 4th century CE <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Christianization of Ireland">Catholicism</a> had begun to gradually subsume or replace the earlier <a href="/wiki/Celtic_polytheism" title="Celtic polytheism">Celtic polytheism</a>. By the end of the 6th century it had introduced writing along with a predominantly monastic <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christian</a> church, profoundly altering Irish society.  <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raids and settlement from the late 8th century CE resulted in extensive cultural interchange, as well as innovation in military and transport technology. Many of Ireland\'s towns were founded at this time as Viking trading posts and coinage made its first appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Viking penetration was limited and concentrated along coasts and rivers, and ceased to be a major threat to Gaelic culture after the battle of Clontarf in 1014.  The <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">Norman invasion</a> in 1169 resulted again in a partial conquest of the island and marked the beginning of more than 800 years of English political and military involvement in Ireland. Initially successful, Norman gains were rolled back over succeeding centuries as a Gaelic resurgence<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> reestablished Gaelic cultural preeminence over most of the country, apart from the walled towns and the area around <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> known as <a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">The Pale</a>.\n</p><p>Reduced to the control of small pockets, the <a href="/wiki/English_Crown" class="mw-redirect" title="English Crown">English Crown</a> did not make another attempt to conquer the island until after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>. This released resources and manpower for overseas expansion, beginning in the early 16th century. Also, the European  <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_America:_Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery of America: Voyages of Christopher Columbus">discovery of America</a> by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> in 1492 meant that Ireland now occupied a position of great importance west of Britain, and therefore controlled the routes from Britain into the Atlantic, and ultimately, America.  However, the nature of Ireland\'s decentralised political organisation into small territories (known as <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BAath" title="T\xc3\xbaath">t\xc3\xbaatha</a>), martial traditions, difficult terrain and climate and lack of urban infrastructure, meant that attempts to assert Crown authority were slow and expensive. Attempts to impose the new Protestant faith were also successfully resisted by both the Gaelic and Norman-Irish. The new policy fomented the rebellion of the <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norman" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno-Norman">Hiberno-Norman</a> <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Kildare" class="mw-redirect" title="Earl of Kildare">Earl of Kildare</a> <a href="/wiki/Silken_Thomas" class="mw-redirect" title="Silken Thomas">Silken Thomas</a> in 1534, keen to defend his traditional autonomy and Catholicism, and marked the beginning of the prolonged <a href="/wiki/Tudor_period" title="Tudor period">Tudor</a> conquest of Ireland lasting from 1534 to 1603. <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> proclaimed himself <a href="/wiki/King_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Ireland">King of Ireland</a> in 1541 to facilitate the project. With the failure of the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a>, Ireland became a battleground in the <a href="/wiki/Religious_wars_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious wars in Europe">wars</a> between Catholic <a href="/wiki/Counter-Reformation" title="Counter-Reformation">Counter-Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> Europe for control of the north Atlantic sea routes to America.\n</p><p>Englands attempts to either conquer or assimilate both the Hiberno-Norman lordships and the Gaelic territories into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> provided the impetus for ongoing warfare, notable examples being the <a href="/wiki/First_Desmond_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="First Desmond Rebellion">1st Desmond Rebellion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Desmond_Rebellion" title="Second Desmond Rebellion">2nd Desmond Rebellion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War_(Ireland)" title="Nine Years&#39; War (Ireland)">Nine Years War</a> . This period was marked by the Crown policies of, at first, <a href="/wiki/Surrender_and_regrant" title="Surrender and regrant">surrender and regrant</a>, and later, <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">plantation</a>, involving the arrival of thousands of English and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> settlers, and the displacement of both the Hiberno-Normans (or Old English as they were known by then) and the native <a href="/wiki/Catholicism_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism in Ireland">Catholic</a> landholders.<a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Ireland</a> was finally defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kinsale" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Kinsale">battle of Kinsale</a> in 1601 which marked the collapse of the Gaelic system and the beginning of Ireland\'s history as part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>.\n</p><p>During the 17th century, this division between a Protestant landholding minority and a dispossessed Catholic majority, divided not only by religion but also by cultural origin, was intensified and conflict between them was to became a recurrent theme in Irish history. Protestant domination of Ireland under a <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a> was reinforced after two periods of religious war, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Confederate_Wars" title="Irish Confederate Wars">Irish Confederate Wars</a> in 1641-52 and the <a href="/wiki/Williamite_War_in_Ireland" title="Williamite War in Ireland">Williamite war</a> in 1689-91. Political power thereafter rested almost exclusively in the hands of a minority <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a>, while Catholics and members of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonconformism">dissenting</a> Protestant denominations suffered severe political and economic privations under the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a>.\n</p><p>On 1 January 1801, in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_republicanism" title="Irish republicanism">republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">United Irishmen Rebellion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_House_of_Commons" title="Irish House of Commons">Irish Parliament</a> was abolished and Ireland became part of a new <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> formed by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>. Catholics were not granted full rights until <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a> in 1829, achieved by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%E2%80%99Connell" class="mw-redirect" title="Daniel O\xe2\x80\x99Connell">Daniel O\xe2\x80\x99Connell</a>. The catastrophe of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a> struck Ireland in 1845 resulting in over a million deaths from starvation and disease and in a million refugees fleeing the country, mainly to America.  Irish attempts to break away continued with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Parnells</a> <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a> which strove from the 1880s to attain <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule Bill">Home Rule</a> through the parliamentary constitutional movement, eventually winning the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Act_1914" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule Act 1914">Home Rule Act 1914</a>, although this Act was suspended at the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.\n</p><p>In 1916 the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> organised by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Brotherhood" title="Irish Republican Brotherhood">IRB</a> and carried out by members of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Irish_Citizen_Army" title="Irish Citizen Army">Irish Citizen Army</a> of <a href="/wiki/James_Connolly" title="James Connolly">James Connolly</a> and 200 women from <a href="/wiki/Cumann_na_mBan" title="Cumann na mBan">Cumann na mBan</a> succeeded in turning public opinion against the British establishment after the execution of the leaders by British authorities. It also eclipsed the home rule movement by bringing <a href="/wiki/Physical_force_republicanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical force republicanism">physical force republicanism</a> back to the forefront of Irish politics.  In 1922, after the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a> most of Ireland seceded from the United Kingdom to become the independent <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> but under the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> the six northeastern counties, known as <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, remained within the United Kingdom, creating the partition of Ireland. The treaty was opposed by many; their opposition led to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Irish Civil War</a>, in which pro-treaty or Free State forces proved victorious. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Ireland" title="History of Northern Ireland">history of Northern Ireland</a> has since been dominated by the division of society along sectarian faultlines and conflict between (mainly Catholic) <a href="/wiki/Irish_Nationalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Nationalism">Irish nationalists</a> and (mainly Protestant) <a href="/wiki/Unionism_in_Ireland" title="Unionism in Ireland">unionists</a>. These divisions erupted into <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a> in the late 1960s, after civil rights marches were met with opposition by authorities. The violence escalated after the deployment of the British Army to restore order led to clashes with nationalist communities. The violence continued for 28 years until an uneasy, but largely successful peace was finally achieved with the <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a> in 1998.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" Britain</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Recent_approaches"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Recent approaches</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">17.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Prehistory_.2810.2C500_BCE.E2.80.93400_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_(10,500_BCE\xe2\x80\x93400_CE)">Prehistory (10,500 BCE\xe2\x80\x93400 CE)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prehistory (10,500 BCE\xe2\x80\x93400 CE)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Ireland" title="Prehistoric Ireland">Prehistoric Ireland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_Age_to_Bronze_Age">Stone Age to Bronze Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Stone Age to Bronze Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif/220px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif/330px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif/440px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="581" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-ice_age.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ireland during the <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a></div></div></div>\n<p>What is known of pre-Christian Ireland comes from references in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> writings, <a href="/wiki/Irish_poetry" title="Irish poetry">Irish poetry</a> and myth, and archaeology. While some possible <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> tools have been found, none of the finds are convincing of Paleolithic settlement in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However a bear bone found in Alice and Gwendoline Cave, County Clare, in 1903 may push back dates for the earliest human settlement of Ireland to 10,500 BCE. The bone shows clear signs of cut marks with stone tools, and has been radiocarbon dated to 12,500 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest confirmed inhabitants of Ireland were <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>, who arrived some time around 7900 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> While some authors take the view that a <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a> connecting Ireland to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> still existed at that time,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> more recent studies indicate that Ireland was separated from Britain by c. 14,000 BCE, when the climate was still cold and local ice caps persisted in parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The people remained hunter-gatherers until about 4000 BCE. It is argued this is when the first signs of agriculture started to show, leading to the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> culture, characterised by the appearance of pottery, polished stone tools, rectangular wooden houses, megalithic tombs, and domesticated sheep and cattle.<sup id="cite_ref-Landscapes_of_Neolithic_Ireland_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landscapes_of_Neolithic_Ireland-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Some of these tombs, as at <a href="/wiki/Knowth" title="Knowth">Knowth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dowth" title="Dowth">Dowth</a>, are huge stone monuments and many of them, such as the <a href="/wiki/Passage_Tombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Passage Tombs">Passage Tombs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Newgrange" title="Newgrange">Newgrange</a>, are astronomically aligned. Four main types of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Megalithic_Tombs" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Megalithic Tombs">Irish Megalithic Tombs</a> have been identified: <a href="/wiki/Dolmen" title="Dolmen">dolmens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Court_cairn" title="Court cairn">court cairns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passage_tomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Passage tomb">passage tombs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wedge-shaped_gallery_grave" class="mw-redirect" title="Wedge-shaped gallery grave">wedge-shaped gallery graves</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Landscapes_of_Neolithic_Ireland_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Landscapes_of_Neolithic_Ireland-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> and Munster, individual adult males were buried in small stone structures, called <a href="/wiki/Cist" title="Cist">cists</a>, under earthen mounds and were accompanied by distinctive decorated pottery. This culture apparently prospered, and the island became more densely populated. Near the end of the Neolithic new types of monuments developed, such as circular embanked enclosures and timber, stone and post and pit circles.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newgrange.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Newgrange.JPG/220px-Newgrange.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Newgrange.JPG/330px-Newgrange.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Newgrange.JPG/440px-Newgrange.JPG 2x" data-file-width="922" data-file-height="692" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newgrange.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Newgrange" title="Newgrange">Newgrange</a>, built c. 3200 BCE, is an Irish <a href="/wiki/Passage_tomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Passage tomb">passage tomb</a> located at <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%BA_na_B%C3%B3inne" title="Br\xc3\xba na B\xc3\xb3inne">Br\xc3\xba na B\xc3\xb3inne</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, which came to Ireland around 2000 BCE, saw the production of elaborate <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> ornaments, weapons and tools. There was a movement away from the construction of communal megalithic tombs to the burial of the dead in small stone cists or simple pits, which could be situated in cemeteries or in circular earth or stone built burial mounds known respectively as <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">barrows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cairn" title="Cairn">cairns</a>. As the period progressed, inhumation burial gave way to cremation and by the Middle Bronze Age, remains were often placed beneath large burial urns.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Iron Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Iron Age in Ireland began about 600 BCE. The period between the start of the Iron Age and the historic period (431 CE) saw the gradual infiltration of small groups of Celtic-speaking people into Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ross_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> with items of the continental Celtic <a href="/wiki/La_Tene_style" class="mw-redirect" title="La Tene style">La Tene style</a> being found in at least the northern part of the island by about 300 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oxford_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The result of a gradual blending of Celtic and indigenous cultures would result in the emergence of Gaelic culture by the fifth century.<sup id="cite_ref-Bardon_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bardon-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sean_Duffy_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sean_Duffy-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It is also during the fifth century that the main over-kingdoms of In Tuisceart, Airgialla, Ulaid, Mide, Laigin, Mumhain, C\xc3\xb3iced Ol nEchmacht began to emerge (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_kingdoms" title="List of Irish kingdoms">Kingdoms of ancient Ireland</a>). Within these kingdoms a rich culture flourished. The society of these kingdoms was dominated by an upper class consisting of aristocratic warriors and learned people, which possibly included <a href="/wiki/Druid" title="Druid">Druids</a>.\n</p><p>Linguists realised from the 17th century onwards that the language spoken by these people, the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a>, was a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>. This is usually explained as a result of invasions by <a href="/wiki/Celt" class="mw-redirect" title="Celt">Celts</a> from the continent. However, other research has postulated that the culture developed gradually and continuously, and that the introduction of Celtic language and elements of Celtic culture may have been a result of cultural exchange with Celtic groups in southwest continental Europe from the Neolithic to the Bronze Age.<sup id="cite_ref-prospect_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prospect-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hnn_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hnn-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The hypothesis that the native Late Bronze Age inhabitants gradually absorbed Celtic influences has since been supported by some recent genetic research.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (October 2012)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The Romans referred to Ireland as Scotia 500 CE, and later <a href="/wiki/Hibernia" title="Hibernia">Hibernia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, in 100 CE, recorded Ireland\'s geography and tribes. Ireland was never a part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, but Roman influence was often projected well beyond its borders. <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> writes that an exiled Irish prince was with <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Agricola</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> and would return to seize power in Ireland. <a href="/wiki/Satires_of_Juvenal" class="mw-redirect" title="Satires of Juvenal">Juvenal</a> tells us that Roman "arms had been taken beyond the shores of Ireland". In recent years, some experts have hypothesized that Roman-sponsored Gaelic forces (or perhaps even Roman regulars) mounted some kind of invasion around 100 CE,<sup id="cite_ref-ba-n014-0596_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ba-n014-0596-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> but the exact relationship between Rome and the dynasties and peoples of Hibernia remains unclear.\n</p><p>Irish confederations (the <a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scoti</a>) attacked and some settled in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Conspiracy" title="Great Conspiracy">Great Conspiracy</a> of 367.  In particular, the <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> settled in western Scotland and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Isles" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Isles">Western Isles</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_Christian_Ireland_.28400.E2.80.93800.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christian_Ireland_(400\xe2\x80\x93800)">Early Christian Ireland (400\xe2\x80\x93800)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early Christian Ireland (400\xe2\x80\x93800)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ireland_(400%E2%80%93800)" title="History of Ireland (400\xe2\x80\x93800)">History of Ireland (400\xe2\x80\x93800)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glendalough_monastery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Glendalough_monastery.jpg/220px-Glendalough_monastery.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Glendalough_monastery.jpg/330px-Glendalough_monastery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Glendalough_monastery.jpg/440px-Glendalough_monastery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glendalough_monastery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kevin_of_Glendalough" title="Kevin of Glendalough">Kevin\'s</a> monastery at <a href="/wiki/Glendalough" title="Glendalough">Glendalough</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Wicklow" title="County Wicklow">County Wicklow</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The middle centuries of the first millennium CE marked great changes in Ireland. Politically, what appears to have been a prehistoric emphasis on tribal affiliation had been replaced by the 8th century by patrilineal dynasties ruling the island\'s kingdoms. Many formerly powerful kingdoms and peoples disappeared. Irish pirates struck all over the coast of western Britain in the same way that the Vikings would later attack Ireland. Some of these founded entirely new kingdoms in Pictland and, to a lesser degree, in parts of Cornwall, Wales, and Cumbria. The <a href="/wiki/Attacotti" title="Attacotti">Attacotti</a> of south <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> may even have served in the Roman military in the mid-to-late 300s.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Perhaps it was some of the latter returning home as rich mercenaries, merchants, or slaves stolen from Britain or Gaul, that first brought the Christian faith to Ireland. Some early sources claim that there were missionaries active in southern Ireland long before <a href="/wiki/St._Patrick" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Patrick">St. Patrick</a>. Whatever the route, and there were probably many, this new faith was to have the most profound effect on the Irish.\n</p><p>Tradition maintains that in A.D. 432, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick" title="Saint Patrick">St. Patrick</a> arrived on the island and, in the years that followed, worked to convert the Irish to Christianity. St Patrick\'s <i>Confession</i>, in Latin, written by him is the earliest Irish historical document. It gives some information about the Saint.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, according to <a href="/wiki/Prosper_of_Aquitaine" title="Prosper of Aquitaine">Prosper of Aquitaine</a>, a contemporary chronicler, <a href="/wiki/Palladius_(bishop_of_Ireland)" title="Palladius (bishop of Ireland)">Palladius</a> was sent to Ireland by the Pope in 431 as <i>"first Bishop to the Irish believing in Christ"</i>, which demonstrates that there were already Christians living in Ireland. Palladius seems to have worked purely as Bishop to Irish Christians in the Leinster and Meath kingdoms, while Patrick \xe2\x80\x93 who may have arrived as late as 461 \xe2\x80\x93 worked first and foremost as a missionary to the pagan Irish, in the more remote kingdoms in Ulster and Connacht.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/220px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/330px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg/440px-KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KellsFol292rIncipJohn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a> that opens the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Patrick is traditionally credited with preserving and codifying Irish laws and changing only those that conflicted with Christian practices. He is credited with introducing the <a href="/wiki/Roman_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman alphabet">Roman alphabet</a>, which enabled Irish monks to preserve parts of the extensive oral literature. The historicity of these claims remains the subject of debate and there is no direct evidence linking Patrick with any of these accomplishments. The myth of Patrick, as scholars refer to it, was developed in the centuries after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Irish scholars excelled in the study of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> learning and Christian theology in the monasteries that flourished shortly thereafter. <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Missionaries from Ireland</a> to England and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> spread news of the flowering of learning, and scholars from other nations came to Irish monasteries. The excellence and isolation of these monasteries helped preserve Latin learning during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>. The period of <a href="/wiki/Insular_art" title="Insular art">Insular art</a>, mainly in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>, metalworking, and sculpture flourished and produced such treasures as the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Kells" title="Book of Kells">Book of Kells</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ardagh_Chalice" class="mw-redirect" title="Ardagh Chalice">Ardagh Chalice</a>, and the many carved <a href="/wiki/High_cross" title="High cross">stone crosses</a> that dot the island. Insular style was to be a crucial ingredient in the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_art" title="Romanesque art">Romanesque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> styles throughout Western Europe. Sites dating to this period include <a href="/wiki/Clochan" class="mw-redirect" title="Clochan">clochans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ringfort" title="Ringfort">ringforts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Promontory_fort" title="Promontory fort">promontory forts</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Francis_John_Byrne" title="Francis John Byrne">Francis John Byrne</a> describes the effect of the epidemics which occurred during this era:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>\nThe plagues of the 660s and the 680s had a traumatic effect on Irish society. The golden age of the saints was over, together with the generation of kings who could fire a <a href="/wiki/Saga" title="Saga">saga</a>-writer\'s imagination. The literary tradition looks back to the reign of the sons of <a href="/wiki/Aed_Slaine" class="mw-redirect" title="Aed Slaine">Aed Slaine</a> (Diarmait and Blathmac, who died in 665) as to the end of an era. Antiquaries, <a href="/wiki/Brehon" title="Brehon">brehons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiographers</a>, felt the need to collect ancient traditions before they were totally forgotten. Many were in fact swallowed by oblivion; when we examine the writing of <a href="/wiki/Tirechan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tirechan">Tirechan</a> we encounter obscure references to tribes which are quite unknown to the later genealogical tradition. The laws describe a ... society that was <a href="/wiki/Obsolescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Obsolescent">obsolescent</a>, and the meaning and use of the word <i>moccu</i><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> dies out with archaic <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> at the beginning of the new century.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The first English involvement in Ireland took place in this period. Tullylease, <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Rathmelsigi" class="mw-redirect" title="Abbey of Rathmelsigi">Rath Melsigi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mayo,_County_Mayo" title="Mayo, County Mayo">Maigh Eo na Saxain</a> were founded by 670 for English students who wished to study or live in Ireland. In summer 684, an English expeditionary force sent by <a href="/wiki/Northumbrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian">Northumbrian</a> King <a href="/wiki/Ecgfrith_of_Northumbria" title="Ecgfrith of Northumbria">Ecgfrith</a> raided Brega.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_medieval_and_Viking_era_.28800.E2.80.931166.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_medieval_and_Viking_era_(800\xe2\x80\x931166)">Early medieval and Viking era (800\xe2\x80\x931166)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early medieval and Viking era (800\xe2\x80\x931166)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_Medieval_Ireland_800%E2%80%931166" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Medieval Ireland 800\xe2\x80\x931166">Early Medieval Ireland 800\xe2\x80\x931166</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brian_boru_scaled.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brian_boru_scaled.jpg/220px-Brian_boru_scaled.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Brian_boru_scaled.jpg/330px-Brian_boru_scaled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Brian_boru_scaled.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="565" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brian_boru_scaled.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Brian_Boru" title="Brian Boru">Brian Boru</a>, Irish hero who fought the Vikings</div></div></div>\n<p>The first recorded<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> raid in Irish history occurred in 795 AD when Vikings from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> looted the island. Early Viking raids were generally fast-paced and small in scale. These early raids interrupted the golden age of Christian Irish culture and marked the beginning of two centuries of intermittent warfare, with waves of Viking raiders plundering monasteries and towns throughout Ireland. Most of those early raiders came from western Norway.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The Vikings were expert sailors, who travelled in <a href="/wiki/Longship" title="Longship">longships</a>, and by the early 840s, had begun to establish settlements along the Irish coasts and to spend the winter months there.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The longships were technologically advanced,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> allowing them to travel faster through the narrow rivers. Vikings founded settlements in several places; most famously in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>. Most of the settlements were near the water, allowing the Vikings to trade using their longships. Written accounts<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> from this time (early to mid 840s) show that the Vikings were moving further inland to attack (often using rivers) and then retreating to their coastal headquarters.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_Ireland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Viking_Ireland.png/220px-Viking_Ireland.png" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/15/Viking_Ireland.png 1.5x" data-file-width="292" data-file-height="274" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Viking_Ireland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the <a href="/wiki/Longphort" title="Longphort">Viking settlements</a> in Ireland</div></div></div>\n<p>In 852, the Vikings landed in <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Bay" title="Dublin Bay">Dublin Bay</a> and established a fortress. Dublin became the centre for trade of many goods, especially slaves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Bringing back new ideas and motivations, they began settling more permanently. In the tenth century an earthen bank was constructed around the city with a second larger bank built outside that in the eleventh century. On the interior of the town, an extensive series of defenses have been excavated at Fishamble Street, Dublin. The site featured nine waterfronts, including two possible flood banks and two positive defensive embankments during the Viking Age. The early embankments were non-defensive, being only one meter high, and it is uncertain how much of the site they encircled. After several generations a group of mixed Irish and Norse ethnic background arose, the <i><a href="/wiki/Norse%E2%80%93Gaels" title="Norse\xe2\x80\x93Gaels">Gall-Gaels</a></i>, \'(<i>Gall</i> being the Old Irish word for foreign).\n</p><p>The second wave of Vikings made stations at winter-bases called <a href="/wiki/Longphort" title="Longphort">longphorts</a> to serve as control centres to exert a more localized force on the island through raiding.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The third wave in 917 established towns as not only control centres, but also as centres of trade to enter into Irish economy and greater Western Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Returning to Dublin, they set up a market town. Over the next century a great period of economic growth would spread across the pastoral country. The Vikings introduced the concept of international trade to the Irish,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2018)">dubious</span></a>&#32;<span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:History_of_Ireland#Dubious" title="Talk:History of Ireland">discuss</a></span></i>&#93;</sup> as well as popularized a silver economy with local trade and the first minting of coins in 997.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 902 <a href="/w/index.php?title=M%C3%A1el_Finnia_mac_Flannacain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="M\xc3\xa1el Finnia mac Flannacain (page does not exist)">M\xc3\xa1el Finnia mac Flannacain</a> of Brega and <a href="/wiki/Cerball_mac_Muirec%C3%A1in" title="Cerball mac Muirec\xc3\xa1in">Cerball mac Muirec\xc3\xa1in</a> of Leinster joined forces against Dublin, and "The heathens were driven from Ireland, i.e. from the fortress of \xc3\x81th Cliath [Dublin]".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> They were allowed by the Saxons to settle in <a href="/wiki/Wirral_Peninsula" title="Wirral Peninsula">Wirral</a>, England, but would however later return to retake Dublin.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Vikings never achieved total domination of Ireland, often fighting for and against various Irish kings. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Clontarf" title="Battle of Clontarf">Battle of Clontarf</a> in 1014 began the decline of Viking power in Ireland but the towns which Vikings had founded continued to flourish, and trade became an important part of the Irish economy.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span id="Norman_Ireland_.281168.E2.80.931535.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Norman_Ireland_(1168\xe2\x80\x931535)">Norman Ireland (1168\xe2\x80\x931535)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Norman Ireland (1168\xe2\x80\x931535)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norman_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Ireland">Norman Ireland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_the_Normans">Arrival of the Normans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Arrival of the Normans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">Norman invasion of Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:194px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ireland_tower_house_near_quin_county_clare.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Ireland_tower_house_near_quin_county_clare.jpg" width="192" height="256" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="192" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ireland_tower_house_near_quin_county_clare.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A tower house near <a href="/wiki/Quin,_County_Clare" title="Quin, County Clare">Quin, County Clare</a>. The Normans consolidated their presence in Ireland by building hundreds of castles and towers such as this.</div></div></div>\n<p>By the 12th century, Ireland was divided politically into a shifting hierarchy of <a href="/wiki/Petty_kingdom" title="Petty kingdom">petty kingdoms</a> and over-kingdoms. Power was exercised by the heads of a few regional dynasties vying against each other for supremacy over the whole island. One of these men, King <a href="/wiki/Diarmait_Mac_Murchada" title="Diarmait Mac Murchada">Diarmait Mac Murchada</a> of <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> was forcibly exiled by the new High King, <a href="/wiki/Ruaidri_mac_Tairrdelbach_Ua_Conchobair" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruaidri mac Tairrdelbach Ua Conchobair">Ruaidri mac Tairrdelbach Ua Conchobair</a> of the Western kingdom of Connacht. Fleeing to <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>, Diarmait obtained permission from <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> to recruit <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> knights to regain his kingdom. The first Norman knight landed in Ireland in 1167, followed by the main forces of Normans, Welsh and <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flemings</a>. Several counties were restored to the control of Diarmait, who named his son-in-law, the Norman <a href="/wiki/Richard_de_Clare,_2nd_Earl_of_Pembroke" title="Richard de Clare, 2nd Earl of Pembroke">Richard de Clare</a>, known as Strongbow, heir to his kingdom. This troubled King Henry, who feared the establishment of a rival Norman state in Ireland. Accordingly, he resolved to establish his authority.  In 1177 Prince <a href="/wiki/John_Lackland" class="mw-redirect" title="John Lackland">John Lackland</a> was made <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord of Ireland">Lord of Ireland</a> by his father <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II of England</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Oxford" title="Council of Oxford">Council of Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the authority of the papal bull <a href="/wiki/Laudabiliter" title="Laudabiliter">Laudabiliter</a> from <a href="/wiki/Adrian_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="Adrian IV">Adrian IV</a>, Henry landed with a large fleet at <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a> in 1171, becoming the first <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> to set foot on Irish soil. Henry awarded his Irish territories to his younger son John with the title <i>Dominus Hiberniae</i> ("Lord of Ireland"). When John unexpectedly succeeded his brother as <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">King John of England</a>, the "<a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a>" fell directly under the English Crown.\n</p>\n<div style="float:left;"><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif/220px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif/330px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif/440px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1014.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ireland in 1014: a patchwork of rival kingdoms</div></div></div></div>\n<div style="float:left;"><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif/220px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif/330px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif/440px-Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Www.wesleyjohnston.com-users-ireland-maps-historical-map1300.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The extent of Norman control of Ireland in 1300</div></div></div></div>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lordship_of_Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lordship of Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a></div>\n<p>The Normans initially controlled the entire east coast, from <a href="/wiki/Waterford" title="Waterford">Waterford</a> to eastern <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, and penetrated a considerable distance inland as well. The counties were ruled by many smaller kings. The first Lord of Ireland was King John, who visited Ireland in 1185 and 1210 and helped consolidate the Norman-controlled areas, while ensuring that the many Irish kings swore fealty to him.\n</p><p>Throughout the thirteenth century the policy of the English Kings was to weaken the power of the Norman Lords in Ireland. For example, King John encouraged <a href="/wiki/Hugh_de_Lacy,_1st_Earl_of_Ulster" title="Hugh de Lacy, 1st Earl of Ulster">Hugh de Lacy</a> to destabilise and then overthrow the Lord of Ulster, before naming him as the first Earl of Ulster. The <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norman" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno-Norman">Hiberno-Norman</a> community suffered from a series of invasions that ceased the spread of their settlement and power. Politics and events in Gaelic Ireland served to draw the settlers deeper into the orbit of the Irish.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaelic_resurgence_and_Norman_decline">Gaelic resurgence and Norman decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gaelic resurgence and Norman decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg/220px-Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg/330px-Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg/440px-Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="979" data-file-height="740" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Durer-Irish-16thC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Irish soldiers, 1521 \xe2\x80\x93 by <a href="/wiki/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer" title="Albrecht D\xc3\xbcrer">Albrecht D\xc3\xbcrer</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>By 1261 the weakening of the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> had become manifest when <a href="/wiki/F%C3%ADnghin_Mac_Carthaigh" title="F\xc3\xadnghin Mac Carthaigh">Fineen MacCarthy</a> defeated a Norman army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Callann" title="Battle of Callann">Battle of Callann</a>. The war continued between the different lords and earls for about 100 years, causing much destruction, especially around Dublin. In this chaotic situation, local Irish lords won back large amounts of land that their families had lost since the conquest and held them after the war was over.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> arrived in Ireland in 1348. Because most of the English and Norman inhabitants of Ireland lived in towns and villages, the plague hit them far harder than it did the native Irish, who lived in more dispersed rural settlements. After it had passed, Gaelic Irish language and customs came to dominate the country again. The English-controlled territory shrank to a fortified area around Dublin (<a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">the Pale</a>), whose rulers had little real authority outside (beyond the Pale).\n</p><p>By the end of the 15th century, central English authority in Ireland had almost disappeared. England\'s attentions were diverted by the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">Lordship of Ireland</a> lay in the hands of the powerful Fitzgerald <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Kildare" class="mw-redirect" title="Earl of Kildare">Earl of Kildare</a>, who dominated the country by means of military force and alliances with Irish lords and clans. Around the country, local Gaelic and <a href="/wiki/Gaelicisation" title="Gaelicisation">Gaelicised</a> lords expanded their powers at the expense of the English government in Dublin but the power of the Dublin government was seriously curtailed by the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Poynings%27_Law" title="Poynings&#39; Law">Poynings\' Law</a> in 1494. According to this act the Irish Parliament was essentially put under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Westminster Parliament</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_modern_Ireland_.281536.E2.80.931691.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Ireland_(1536\xe2\x80\x931691)">Early modern Ireland (1536\xe2\x80\x931691)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early modern Ireland (1536\xe2\x80\x931691)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ireland_1536%E2%80%931691" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland 1536\xe2\x80\x931691">Ireland 1536\xe2\x80\x931691</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_and_rebellion">Conquest and rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Conquest and rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">Tudor conquest of Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a></div>\n<p>From 1536, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a> decided to conquer Ireland and bring it under crown control. The Fitzgerald dynasty of <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Kildare" class="mw-redirect" title="Earl of Kildare">Kildare</a>, who had become the effective rulers of Ireland in the 15th century, had become unreliable allies of the Tudor monarchs. They had invited <a href="/wiki/Burgundians" title="Burgundians">Burgundian</a> troops into Dublin to crown the <a href="/wiki/Yorkist" class="mw-redirect" title="Yorkist">Yorkist</a> pretender, <a href="/wiki/Lambert_Simnel" title="Lambert Simnel">Lambert Simnel</a> as <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> in 1487. Again in 1536, <a href="/wiki/Silken_Thomas" class="mw-redirect" title="Silken Thomas">Silken Thomas</a> Fitzgerald went into open rebellion against the crown. Having put down this rebellion, Henry resolved to bring Ireland under English government control so the island would not become a base for future rebellions or foreign invasions of England. In 1541, he upgraded Ireland from a lordship to a full <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom</a>. Henry was proclaimed King of Ireland at a meeting of the Irish Parliament that year. This was the first meeting of the Irish Parliament to be attended by the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Ireland" title="Gaelic Ireland">Gaelic Irish</a> chieftains as well as the <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norman" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno-Norman">Hiberno-Norman</a> aristocracy. With the institutions of government in place, the next step was to extend the control of the English Kingdom of Ireland over all of its claimed territory. This took nearly a century, with various English administrations either negotiating or fighting with the independent Irish and Old English lords. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada_in_Ireland" title="Spanish Armada in Ireland">Spanish Armada in Ireland</a> suffered heavy losses during an extraordinary season of storms in the autumn of 1588. Among the survivors was Captain <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Cuellar" title="Francisco de Cuellar">Francisco de Cuellar</a>, who gave a remarkable account of his experiences on the run in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The re-conquest was completed during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Elizabeth</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I</a>, after several brutal conflicts. (See the <a href="/wiki/Desmond_Rebellions" title="Desmond Rebellions">Desmond Rebellions</a>, 1569\xe2\x80\x9373 and 1579\xe2\x80\x9383, and the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years_War_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nine Years War (Ireland)">Nine Years War</a>, 1594\xe2\x80\x931603, for details.) After this point, the English authorities in Dublin established real control over Ireland for the first time, bringing a centralised government to the entire island, and successfully disarmed the native lordships. In 1614 the Catholic majority in the Irish Parliament was overthrown through the creation of numerous new boroughs which were dominated by the new settlers. However, the English were not successful in converting the Catholic Irish to the Protestant religion and the brutal methods used by crown authority (including resorting to <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a>) to bring the country under English control, heightened resentment of English rule.\n</p><p>From the mid-16th to the early 17th century, crown governments had carried out a policy of land confiscation and <a href="/wiki/Colonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonisation">colonisation</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Plantations_of_Ireland" title="Plantations of Ireland">Plantations</a>. Scottish and English Protestant colonists were sent to the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> and the counties of <a href="/wiki/Laois" class="mw-redirect" title="Laois">Laois</a> and <a href="/wiki/Offaly" class="mw-redirect" title="Offaly">Offaly</a>. These Protestant settlers replaced the Irish Catholic landowners who were removed from their lands. These settlers formed the ruling class of future British appointed administrations in Ireland. Several <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal Laws">Penal Laws</a>, aimed at Catholics, Baptists and Presbyterians, were introduced to encourage conversion to the established (<a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>) <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_and_penal_laws">Wars and penal laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Wars and penal laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/220px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/330px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg/440px-Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oliver_Cromwell_by_Samuel_Cooper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After an unusually bitter Irish Catholic rebellion and civil war, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, on behalf of the English Commonwealth, re-conquered Ireland by invasion which lasted from 1649 to 1651. Under Cromwell\'s government, landownership in Ireland was transferred overwhelmingly to <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> soldiery and commercial undertakers to pay for the war.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 17th century was perhaps the bloodiest in Ireland\'s history. Two periods of war (1641\xe2\x80\x9353 and 1689\xe2\x80\x9391) caused huge loss of life. The ultimate dispossession of most of the Irish Catholic landowning class was engineered, and recusants were subordinated under the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a>.\n</p><p>During the 17th century, Ireland was convulsed by <a href="/wiki/Irish_Confederate_Wars" title="Irish Confederate Wars">eleven years of warfare</a>, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">Rebellion of 1641</a>, when Irish Catholics rebelled against the domination of English and Protestant settlers. The Catholic gentry briefly ruled the country as <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Ireland" title="Confederate Ireland">Confederate Ireland</a> (1642\xe2\x80\x931649) against the background of the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a> until <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> <a href="/wiki/Cromwellian_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Cromwellian conquest of Ireland">reconquered</a> Ireland in 1649\xe2\x80\x931653 on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/English_Commonwealth" class="mw-redirect" title="English Commonwealth">English Commonwealth</a>. Cromwell\'s conquest was the most brutal phase of the war. By its close, up to more than a half of Ireland\'s pre-war population was killed or exiled as slaves, where many died due to harsh conditions. As retribution for the rebellion of 1641, the better-quality remaining lands owned by Irish Catholics were confiscated and <a href="/wiki/Cromwellian_Plantation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cromwellian Plantation">given to British settlers</a>. Several hundred remaining native landowners were transplanted to <a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connacht</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/220px-King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg" width="220" height="363" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/330px-King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/440px-King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3963" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_James_II_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II of England</a> by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Godfrey_Kneller" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Godfrey Kneller">Sir Godfrey Kneller</a>.<br />Forty years later, Irish Catholics, known as "Jacobites", fought for James from 1688 to 1691, but failed to restore James to the throne of Ireland, England and Scotland.</div></div></div>\n<p>Ireland became the main battleground after the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688, when the Catholic <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a> left London and the <a href="/wiki/Convention_Parliament_(1689)" title="Convention Parliament (1689)">English Parliament</a> replaced him with <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William of Orange</a>. The wealthier Irish Catholics backed James to try to reverse the <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a> and land confiscations, whereas Protestants supported William and Mary in this "Glorious Revolution" to preserve their property in the country. James and William fought for the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Williamite_war_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Williamite war in Ireland">Williamite War</a>, most famously at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Boyne" title="Battle of the Boyne">Battle of the Boyne</a> in 1690, where James\'s outnumbered forces were defeated.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From the 15th to the 18th century, Irish, English, Scots and Welsh prisoners were transported for forced labor in the Caribbean to work off their term of punishment.  Even larger numbers came voluntarily as indentured servants.  In the 18th century they were sent to the American colonies, and in the early 19th century to Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The Irish were dehumanised by the English, described as "savages," so making their displacement appear all the more justified. In 1654 the British parliament gave <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> a free hand to banish Irish "undesirables".  Cromwell rounded up Catholics throughout the Irish countryside and placed them on ships bound for the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, mainly the island of <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>. By 1655, 12,000 political prisoners had been forcibly shipped to Barbados and into indentured servitude.<sup id="cite_ref-Ph.D.1997_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ph.D.1997-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Protestant_ascendancy_.281691.E2.80.931801.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_ascendancy_(1691\xe2\x80\x931801)">Protestant ascendancy (1691\xe2\x80\x931801)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Protestant ascendancy (1691\xe2\x80\x931801)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ireland_1691%E2%80%931801" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland 1691\xe2\x80\x931801">Ireland 1691\xe2\x80\x931801</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">Protestant Ascendancy</a></div>\n<p>The majority of the people of Ireland were Catholic peasants; they were very poor and largely inert politically during the eighteenth century, as many of their leaders converted to Protestantism to avoid severe economic and political penalties. Nevertheless, there was a growing Catholic cultural awakening underway.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  There were two Protestant groups.  The Presbyterians in Ulster in the North lived in much better economic conditions, but had virtually no political power. Power was held by a small group of Anglo-Irish families, who were  loyal to the Anglican Church of Ireland.  They owned the great bulk of the farmland, where the work was done by the Catholic peasants.  Many of these families lived in England and were absentee landlords, whose loyalty was basically to England.  The Anglo-Irish who lived in Ireland became increasingly identified as Irish nationalists, and were resentful of the English control of their island.  Their spokesmen, such as <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a>, sought more local control.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobite</a> resistance in Ireland was finally ended after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aughrim" title="Battle of Aughrim">Battle of Aughrim</a> in July 1691. The <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws_(Ireland)" title="Penal Laws (Ireland)">Penal Laws</a> that had been relaxed somewhat after the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(1660)" title="Restoration (1660)">Restoration</a> were reinforced more thoroughly after this war, as the infant <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> Ascendency wanted to ensure that the Irish Roman Catholics would not be in a position to repeat their rebellions. Power was held by the 5% who were Protestants belonging to the Church of Ireland. They controlled  all major sectors of the Irish economy, the bulk of the farmland, the \nlegal system, local government and held strong majorities in both houses of the Irish Parliament.  They strongly distrusted the Presbyterians in Ulster, and were convinced that the Catholics should have minimal rights.   They did not have full political control because the government in London had superior authority and treated Ireland like a backward colony.  When the American colonies revolted in the 1770s, the Ascendency wrested multiple concessions to strengthen its power. They did not seek independence because they knew they were heavily outnumbered and ultimately depended upon the British Army to guarantee their security.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Subsequent Irish antagonism toward England was aggravated by the economic situation of Ireland in the 18th century. Some <a href="/wiki/Absentee_landlord" title="Absentee landlord">absentee landlords</a> managed their estates inefficiently, and food tended to be produced for export rather than for domestic consumption. Two very cold winters near the end of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a> led directly to a <a href="/wiki/Irish_Famine_(1740%E2%80%931741)" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Famine (1740\xe2\x80\x931741)">famine between 1740 and 1741</a>, which killed about 400,000 people and caused over 150,000 Irish to leave the island.
