In addition, Irish exports were reduced by the <a href="/wiki/Navigation_Acts" title="Navigation Acts">Navigation Acts</a> from the 1660s, which placed tariffs on Irish products entering England, but exempted English goods from tariffs on entering Ireland. Despite this most of the 18th century was relatively peaceful in comparison with the preceding two centuries, and the population doubled to over four million.\n</p><p>By the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> ruling class had come to see Ireland, not England, as their native country.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> A Parliamentary faction led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Grattan" title="Henry Grattan">Henry Grattan</a> agitated for a more favourable trading relationship with Great Britain and for greater legislative independence for the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Irish Parliament</a>. However, reform in Ireland stalled over the more radical proposals toward <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">enfranchising Irish Catholics</a>. This was partially enabled in 1793, but Catholics could not yet become members of the Irish Parliament, or become government officials. Some were attracted to the more militant example of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of 1789.\n</p><p>Presbyterians and Dissenters too faced persecution on a lesser scale, and in 1791 a group of dissident Protestant individuals, all of whom but two were Presbyterians, held the first meeting of what would become the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_United_Irishmen" class="mw-redirect" title="Society of the United Irishmen">Society of the United Irishmen</a>. Originally they sought to reform the Irish Parliament which was controlled by those belonging to the state church; seek Catholic Emancipation; and help remove religion from politics. When their ideals seemed unattainable they became more determined to use force to overthrow British rule and found a non-sectarian republic. Their activity culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, which was bloodily suppressed.\n</p><p>Ireland was a separate kingdom ruled by King George III of Britain; he set policy for Ireland through his appointment of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Lieutenant_of_Ireland" title="Lord Lieutenant of Ireland">Lord Lieutenant of Ireland</a> or viceroy. In practice, the viceroys lived in England and the affairs in the island were largely controlled by an elite group of Irish Protestants known as "undertakers."  The system changed in 1767, with the appointment of an English politician who became a very strong Viceroy. George Townshend served 1767-72 and was in residence in The Castle in Dublin. Townsend had the strong support of both the King and the British cabinet in London, and all major decisions were basically made in London.  The Ascendancy complained, and obtained a series of new laws in the 1780s that made the Irish Parliament effective and independent of the British Parliament, although still under the supervision of the king and his Privy Council.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Largely in response to the 1798 rebellion, Irish self-government was ended altogether by the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a> (which abolished the Irish Parliament of that era).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Union_with_Great_Britain_.281801.E2.80.931912.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_Great_Britain_(1801\xe2\x80\x931912)">Union with Great Britain (1801\xe2\x80\x931912)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Union with Great Britain (1801\xe2\x80\x931912)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ireland_1801%E2%80%931922" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland 1801\xe2\x80\x931922">Ireland 1801\xe2\x80\x931922</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg/220px-Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg" width="220" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg/330px-Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_O%27Connell_-_Project_Gutenberg_13103.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1800, following the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, the Irish and the British parliaments enacted the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union</a>. The merger created a new political entity called <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> with effect from 1 January 1801. Part of the agreement forming the basis of union was that the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test Act</a> would be repealed to remove any remaining discrimination against Roman Catholics, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a> and other dissenter religions in the newly United Kingdom. However, King <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a>, invoking the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement 1701</a> controversially and adamantly blocked attempts by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>. Pitt resigned in protest, but his successor <a href="/wiki/Henry_Addington" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Addington">Henry Addington</a> and his new cabinet failed to legislate to repeal or change the <a href="/wiki/Test_Act" title="Test Act">Test Act</a>. This was followed by the first <a href="/wiki/Irish_Reform_Act_1832" title="Irish Reform Act 1832">Irish Reform Act 1832</a>, which allowed Catholic members of parliament but raised the property qualification to \xc2\xa310 effectively removing the poorer Irish freeholders from <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">the franchise</a>.\n</p><p>In 1823 an enterprising Catholic lawyer, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_O%27Connell" title="Daniel O&#39;Connell">Daniel O\'Connell</a>, known in Ireland as \'The Liberator\' began an ultimately successful Irish campaign to achieve emancipation, and to be seated in the Parliament. This culminated in O\'Connell\'s successful election in the Clare by-election, which revived the parliamentary efforts at reform.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Relief_Act_1829" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Relief Act 1829">Catholic Relief Act 1829</a> was eventually approved by the UK parliament under the leadership of the Dublin-born Prime Minister, the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington</a>. This indefatigable Anglo-Irish statesman, a former Chief Secretary for Ireland, and hero of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, successfully guided the legislation through both houses of Parliament. By threatening to resign, he persuaded King George IV to sign the bill into law in 1829. The continuing obligation of Roman Catholics to fund the established <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>, however, led to the sporadic skirmishes of the <a href="/wiki/Tithe_War" title="Tithe War">Tithe War</a> of 1831\xe2\x80\x9338. The Church was disestablished by the <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone government</a> in 1867. The continuing enactment of parliamentary reform during the ensuing administrations further extended the initially limited franchise. Daniel O\'Connell M.P. later led the <a href="/wiki/Repeal_Association" title="Repeal Association">Repeal Association</a> in an unsuccessful campaign to undo the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Irish_Famine" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Irish Famine">Great Irish Famine</a> (<i>An Gorta M\xc3\xb3r</i>) was the second of Ireland\'s "Great Famines". It struck the country during 1845\xe2\x80\x9349, with <a href="/wiki/Potato_blight" class="mw-redirect" title="Potato blight">potato blight</a>, exacerbated by the political factors of the time<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> leading to mass starvation and emigration. The impact of emigration in Ireland was severe; the population dropped from over 8 million before the Famine to 4.4 million in 1911. <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Gaelic</a> or Irish, once the island\'s spoken language, declined in use sharply in the nineteenth century as a result of the Famine and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/National_school_(Ireland)" title="National school (Ireland)">National School</a> education system, as well as hostility to the language from leading Irish politicians of the time; it was largely replaced by English.\n</p><p>Outside mainstream nationalism, a series of violent rebellions by Irish republicans took place in 1803, under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Emmet" title="Robert Emmet">Robert Emmet</a>; in 1848 a <a href="/wiki/Young_Irelander_Rebellion_of_1848" title="Young Irelander Rebellion of 1848">rebellion</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Young_Irelanders" class="mw-redirect" title="Young Irelanders">Young Irelanders</a>, most prominent among them, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Francis_Meagher" title="Thomas Francis Meagher">Thomas Francis Meagher</a>; and in 1867, <a href="/wiki/Fenian_Rising" title="Fenian Rising">another insurrection</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Brotherhood" title="Irish Republican Brotherhood">Irish Republican Brotherhood</a>. All failed, but <i>physical force nationalism</i> remained an undercurrent in the nineteenth century.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg/220px-Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg/330px-Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg/440px-Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg 2x" data-file-width="698" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_evicted_by_their_landlord_during_the_Irish_Land_War_c1879.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Irish family evicted at Moyasta, County Clare during <a href="/wiki/Land_War" title="Land War">Land War</a>, c.1879</div></div></div>\n<p>A central issue throughout the 19th and early 20th century was land ownership. A small group of about 10,000 English families owned practically all the farmland; Most were permanent residents of England, and seldom presented the land. They rented it out to Irish tenant farmers.  Falling behind in rent payments meant eviction, and very bad feelings \xe2\x80\x93 often violence.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  The late 19th century witnessed major land reform, spearheaded by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Land_League" title="Irish National Land League">Land League</a> under <a href="/wiki/Michael_Davitt" title="Michael Davitt">Michael Davitt</a> demanding what became known as the <i>3 Fs; Fair rent, free sale, fixity of tenure</i>. Parliament passed laws in 1870, 1881, 1903 and 1909 that enabled most tenant farmers to purchase their lands, and lowered the rents of the others.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> From 1870 and as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Land_War" title="Land War">Land War</a> agitations and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Plan_of_Campaign" title="Plan of Campaign">Plan of Campaign</a> of the 1880s, various British governments introduced a series of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Land_Acts" title="Irish Land Acts">Irish Land Acts</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_O%27Brien" title="William O&#39;Brien">William O\'Brien</a> played a leading role in the 1902 <a href="/wiki/Land_Conference" title="Land Conference">Land Conference</a> to pave the way for the most advanced social legislation in Ireland since the Union, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Land_Acts#Wyndham_Land_(Purchase)_Act_1903" title="Irish Land Acts">Wyndham Land Purchase Act</a> of 1903. This Act set the conditions for the break-up of large estates and gradually devolved to rural landholders, and tenants\' ownership of the lands. It effectively ended the era of the <a href="/wiki/Absentee_landlord" title="Absentee landlord">absentee landlord</a>, finally resolving the Irish Land Question.\n</p><p>In the 1870s the issue of Irish self-government again became a major focus of debate under <a href="/wiki/Charles_Stewart_Parnell" title="Charles Stewart Parnell">Charles Stewart Parnell</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Parliamentary Party</a>. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">Gladstone</a> made two unsuccessful attempts to pass <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule Movement">Home Rule</a> in 1886 and 1893. Parnell\'s leadership ended when he was implicated in a divorce scandal that gained international publicity in 1890. He had been secretly living for years with <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Parnell" class="mw-redirect" title="Katherine Parnell">Katherine O\'Shea</a>, the long-separated wife of a fellow Irish MP. Disaster came quickly: Gladstone and the Liberal Party refused to cooperate with him; his party split; the Irish Catholic bishops led the successful effort to crush his minority faction at by-elections.  Parnell fought for control to the end, but his body was collapsing and he died in 1891 at age 45.\n</p><p>After the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_(Ireland)_Act_1898" title="Local Government (Ireland) Act 1898">Local Government (Ireland) Act 1898</a> which broke the power of the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Ascendancy" title="Protestant Ascendancy">landlord-dominated</a> "Grand Juries", passing for the first time democratic control of local affairs into the hands of the people through elected Local County Councils, the debate over full Home Rule led to tensions between <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish nationalist">Irish nationalists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unionists_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionists (Ireland)">Irish unionists</a> (those who favoured maintenance of the Union). Most of the island was predominantly nationalist, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Catholic</a> and agrarian. The northeast, however, was predominantly unionist, Protestant and industrialised. Unionists feared a loss of political power and economic wealth in a predominantly rural, nationalist, Catholic home-rule state. Nationalists believed they would remain economically and politically second-class citizens without self-government. Out of this division, two opposing sectarian movements evolved, the Protestant <i><a href="/wiki/Orange_Order" title="Orange Order">Orange Order</a></i> and the Catholic <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Order_of_Hibernians" title="Ancient Order of Hibernians">Ancient Order of Hibernians</a></i>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Home_Rule.2C_Easter_Rising_and_War_of_Independence_.281912.E2.80.931922.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_Rule,_Easter_Rising_and_War_of_Independence_(1912\xe2\x80\x931922)">Home Rule, Easter Rising and War of Independence (1912\xe2\x80\x931922)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Home Rule, Easter Rising and War of Independence (1912\xe2\x80\x931922)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_revolutionary_period" title="Irish revolutionary period">Irish revolutionary period</a></div>\n<p>Home Rule became certain when in 1910 the Irish Parliamentary Party (IPP) under <a href="/wiki/John_Redmond" title="John Redmond">John Redmond</a> held the balance of power in <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">Commons</a> and the third Home Rule Bill was introduced in 1912. Unionist resistance was immediate with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteers" title="Ulster Volunteers">Ulster Volunteers</a>. In turn the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Volunteers" title="Irish Volunteers">Irish Volunteers</a> were established to oppose them and enforce the introduction of self-government.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/220px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/330px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png/440px-Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png 2x" data-file-width="954" data-file-height="1450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Easter_Proclamation_of_1916.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Proclamation_of_the_Irish_Republic" title="Proclamation of the Irish Republic">Easter Proclamation</a>, issued by Leaders of the Easter Rising</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1914, just as the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> broke out, the UK Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule_Act_1914" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule Act 1914">Third Home Rule Act</a> to establish self-government for Ireland, but was suspended for the duration of the war. To ensure implementation of Home Rule after the war, nationalist leaders and the IPP under Redmond supported with <a href="/wiki/Ireland_and_World_War_I" title="Ireland and World War I">Ireland\'s participation</a> in the British and <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied</a> war effort under the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Triple Entente</a> against the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>. The core of the Irish Volunteers were against this decision, but the majority left to form the <a href="/wiki/National_Volunteers" title="National Volunteers">National Volunteers</a> who enlisted in <a href="/wiki/Irish_military_diaspora#&#39;Irish&#39;_named_units_of_the_British_Army" title="Irish military diaspora">Irish regiments</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">New British Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/10th_(Irish)_Division" title="10th (Irish) Division">10th</a> and <a href="/wiki/16th_(Irish)_Division" title="16th (Irish) Division">16th (Irish) Divisions</a>, their Northern counterparts in the <a href="/wiki/36th_(Ulster)_Division" title="36th (Ulster) Division">36th (Ulster) Division</a>. Before the war ended, Britain made two concerted efforts to implement Home Rule, one in May 1916 and again with the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Convention" title="Irish Convention">Irish Convention</a> during 1917\xe2\x80\x931918, but the Irish sides (Nationalist, Unionist) were unable to agree to terms for the temporary or permanent exclusion of Ulster from its provisions.\n</p><p>The period 1916\xe2\x80\x931921 was marked by political violence and upheaval, ending in the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">partition of Ireland</a> and independence for 26 of its 32 counties. A failed militant attempt was made to gain separate independence for Ireland with the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a>, an insurrection in Dublin. Though support for the insurgents was small, the violence used in its suppression led to a swing in support of the rebels. In addition, the unprecedented <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">threat of Irishmen being conscripted</a> to the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> in 1918 (for service on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> as a result of the German <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a>) accelerated this change. In the <a href="/wiki/Irish_(UK)_general_election,_1918" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish (UK) general election, 1918">December 1918 elections</a> <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a>, the party of the rebels, won three-quarters of all seats in Ireland, twenty-seven <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MPs</a> of which assembled in Dublin on 21 January 1919 to form a 32-county <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Irish Republic</a> Parliament, the first <a href="/wiki/First_D%C3%A1il" title="First D\xc3\xa1il">D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann</a> <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Independence_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration of Independence (Ireland)">unilaterally declaring</a> sovereignty over the entire island.\n</p>\n<table style="margin: 0 0 1em 1em; border: 1px solid #8888aa; background: #f7f8ff; clear:right; float:right;" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th class="navbox-title" style="padding: 0 5px 0 5px; txt-align: center">Irish parliaments\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div 1800)</small></div></div></div></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IrishHC1780.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/IrishHC1780.jpg/120px-IrishHC1780.jpg" width="120" height="91" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IrishHC1780.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><small>House of Commons of the Kingdom of Ireland (abolished 1800)</small></div></div></div></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leinsterhouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Leinsterhouse.jpg/120px-Leinsterhouse.jpg" width="120" height="89" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Leinsterhouse.jpg/180px-Leinsterhouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Leinsterhouse.jpg/240px-Leinsterhouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="490" data-file-height="365" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leinsterhouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><small><a href="/wiki/Leinster_House" title="Leinster House">Leinster House</a>, home of the Ireland\'s parliament since 1922.</small></div></div></div></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg/120px-Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg" width="120" height="83" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg/180px-Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg/240px-Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2694" data-file-height="1874" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parliament_Buildings_Stormont_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><small><a href="/wiki/Parliament_Buildings_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Parliament Buildings (Northern Ireland)">Parliament Buildings (Stormont)</a>. Previously home of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Parliament</a>. Now used by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Assembly" title="Northern Ireland Assembly">Assembly</a>.</small></div></div></div></div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Unwilling to negotiate any understanding with Britain short of complete independence, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army" title="Irish Republican Army">Irish Republican Army</a>, the army of the newly declared Irish Republic, waged a guerilla war (the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a>) from 1919 to 1921. In the course of the fighting and amid much acrimony, the Fourth <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1920" title="Government of Ireland Act 1920">Government of Ireland Act 1920</a> implemented Home Rule while separating the island into what the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">British government</a>\'s Act termed "<a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Southern_Ireland_(1921%E2%80%9322)" title="Southern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x9322)">Southern Ireland</a>". In July 1921 the Irish and British governments agreed to a truce that halted the war. In December 1921 representatives of both governments signed an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a>. The Irish delegation was led by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Griffith" title="Arthur Griffith">Arthur Griffith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Collins_(Irish_leader)" title="Michael Collins (Irish leader)">Michael Collins</a>. This abolished the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Irish Republic</a> and created the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, a self-governing <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">Dominion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> in the manner of Canada and Australia. Under the Treaty, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> could opt out of the Free State and stay within the United Kingdom: it promptly did so. In 1922 both parliaments <a href="/wiki/Second_D%C3%A1il#The_Treaty" title="Second D\xc3\xa1il">ratified the Treaty</a>, formalising independence for the 26-county Irish Free State (which renamed itself <i>Ireland</i> in 1937, and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Ireland Act">declared itself a republic</a> in 1949); while the 6-county Northern Ireland, gaining Home Rule for itself, remained part of the United Kingdom. For most of the next 75 years, each territory was strongly aligned to either <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Catholic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> ideologies, although this was more marked in the six counties of Northern Ireland.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Free_State_and_Republic_.281922.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_State_and_Republic_(1922\xe2\x80\x93present)">Free State and Republic (1922\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Free State and Republic (1922\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="History of the Republic of Ireland">History of the Republic of Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Irish_state" title="Names of the Irish state">Names of the Irish state</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ei-map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ei-map.svg/220px-Ei-map.svg.png" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ei-map.svg/330px-Ei-map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Ei-map.svg/440px-Ei-map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="756" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ei-map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Political map of Ireland</div></div></div>\n<p>The treaty to sever the Union divided the republican movement into <i>anti-Treaty</i> (who wanted to fight on until an Irish Republic was achieved) and <i>pro-Treaty</i> supporters (who accepted the Free State as a first step towards full independence and unity). Between 1922 and 1923 both sides fought the bloody <a href="/wiki/Irish_Civil_War" title="Irish Civil War">Irish Civil War</a>. The new Irish Free State government defeated the anti-Treaty remnant of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army" title="Irish Republican Army">Irish Republican Army</a>, imposing <a href="/wiki/Executions_during_the_Irish_Civil_War" title="Executions during the Irish Civil War">multiple executions</a>. This division among nationalists still colours Irish politics today, specifically between the two leading Irish political parties, <a href="/wiki/Fianna_F%C3%A1il" title="Fianna F\xc3\xa1il">Fianna F\xc3\xa1il</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fine_Gael" title="Fine Gael">Fine Gael</a>.\n</p><p>The new Irish Free State (1922\xe2\x80\x9337) existed against the backdrop of the growth of dictatorships in mainland Europe and a major <a href="/wiki/Great_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Great depression">world economic downturn</a> in 1929. In contrast with many contemporary European states it remained a democracy. Testament to this came when the losing faction in the Irish civil war, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89amon_de_Valera" title="\xc3\x89amon de Valera">\xc3\x89amon de Valera</a>\'s Fianna F\xc3\xa1il, was able to take power peacefully by winning the <a href="/wiki/Irish_general_election,_1932" title="Irish general election, 1932">1932 general election</a>. Nevertheless, until the mid-1930s, considerable parts of Irish society saw the Free State through the prism of the civil war, as a repressive, British-imposed state. It was only the peaceful change of government in 1932 that signalled the final acceptance of the Free State on their part. In contrast to many other states in the period, the Free State remained financially solvent as a result of low government expenditure, despite the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Trade_War" title="Anglo-Irish Trade War">Economic War</a> with Britain. However, unemployment and emigration were high. The population declined to a low of 2.7 million recorded in the 1961 census.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> had a powerful influence over the Irish state for much of its history. The clergy\'s influence meant that the Irish state had very conservative social policies, forbidding, for example, divorce, <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a>, abortion, pornography as well as encouraging the <a href="/wiki/Censorship_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Censorship in the Republic of Ireland">censoring and banning</a> of many books and films. In addition the Church largely controlled the State\'s hospitals, schools and remained the largest provider of many other social services.\n</p><p>With the partition of Ireland in 1922, 92.6% of the Free State\'s population were Catholic while 7.4% were Protestant.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> By the 1960s the Protestant population had fallen by half. Although emigration was high among all the population, due to a lack of economic opportunity, the rate of Protestant emigration was disproportionate in this period. Many Protestants left the country in the early 1920s, either because they felt unwelcome in a predominantly Catholic and nationalist state, because they were afraid due to the burning of Protestant homes (particularly of the old landed class) by republicans during the civil war, because they regarded themselves as British and did not wish to live in an independent Irish state, or because of the economic disruption caused by the recent violence. The Catholic Church had also issued a decree, known as <a href="/wiki/Ne_Temere" title="Ne Temere">Ne Temere</a>, whereby the children of marriages between Catholics and Protestants had to be brought up as Catholics. From 1945, the emigration rate of Protestants fell and they became less likely to emigrate than Catholics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy,_motorcade,_spectators._Dublin,_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg/220px-President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg/330px-President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg/440px-President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy%2C_motorcade%2C_spectators._Dublin%2C_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2963" data-file-height="2015" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President%27s_Trip_to_Europe-_Motorcade_in_Dublin._President_Kennedy,_motorcade,_spectators._Dublin,_Ireland_-_NARA_-_194227.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> in motorcade in <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a> on 27 June 1963</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1937 a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution</a> re-established the state as <i>Ireland</i> (or <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%89ire" title="\xc3\x89ire">\xc3\x89ire</a></i> in Irish). The state remained neutral throughout <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Irish_neutrality" title="Irish neutrality">Irish neutrality</a></i>), which saved it from much of the horrors of the war, although tens of thousands volunteered to serve in the British forces. Ireland was also impacted by food rationing, and coal shortages; <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B3rd_na_M%C3%B3na" class="mw-redirect" title="B\xc3\xb3rd na M\xc3\xb3na">peat production</a> became a priority during this time. Though nominally neutral, recent studies have suggested a far greater level of involvement by the South with the Allies than was realised, with <a href="/wiki/D_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="D Day">D Day</a>\'s date set on the basis of secret weather information on Atlantic storms supplied by Ireland. <i>For more detail on 1939\xe2\x80\x9345, see main article <a href="/wiki/The_Emergency_(Ireland)" title="The Emergency (Ireland)">The Emergency</a></i>.\n</p><p>In 1949 the state was formally declared a republic and it left the British <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>.\n</p><p>In the 1960s, Ireland underwent a major economic change under reforming <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a> (prime minister) <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_Lemass" title="Se\xc3\xa1n Lemass">Se\xc3\xa1n Lemass</a> and Secretary of the Department of Finance <a href="/wiki/T.K._Whitaker" class="mw-redirect" title="T.K. Whitaker">T.K. Whitaker</a>, who produced a series of economic plans. Free second-level education was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Donogh_O%27Malley" title="Donogh O&#39;Malley">Donogh O\'Malley</a> as Minister for Education in 1968. From the early 1960s, Ireland sought admission to the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> but, because 90% of exports were to the United Kingdom market, it did not do so until the UK did, in 1973.\n</p><p>Global economic problems in the 1970s, augmented by a set of misjudged economic policies followed by governments, including that of Taoiseach <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lynch" title="Jack Lynch">Jack Lynch</a>, caused the Irish economy to stagnate. <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">The Troubles</a> in Northern Ireland discouraged foreign investment. Devaluation was enabled when the Irish Pound, or Punt, was established as a separate currency in 1979, breaking the link with the UK\'s <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">sterling</a>. However, economic reforms in the late 1980s, helped by investment from the <a href="/wiki/European_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community">European Community</a>, led to the emergence of one of the world\'s highest economic growth rates, with mass immigration (particularly of people from Asia and Eastern Europe) as a feature of the late 1990s. This period came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Tiger" title="Celtic Tiger">Celtic Tiger</a> and was focused on as a model for economic development in the former Eastern Bloc states, which entered the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in the early 2000s (decade). Property values had risen by a factor of between four and ten between 1993 and 2006, in part fuelling the boom.\n</p><p>Irish society adopted relatively liberal social policies during this period. Divorce was legalised, homosexuality decriminalised, and abortion in limited cases was allowed by the Irish Supreme Court in the <a href="/wiki/X_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="X Case">X Case</a> legal judgement. Major scandals in the Roman Catholic Church, both sexual and financial, coincided with a widespread decline in religious practice, with weekly attendance at Roman Catholic <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a> dropping by half in twenty years. <a href="/wiki/Public_inquiries_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Public inquiries in the Republic of Ireland">A series of tribunals</a> set up from the 1990s have investigated alleged malpractices by politicians, the Catholic clergy, judges, hospitals and the Garda\xc3\xad (police).\n</p><p>Ireland\'s new found prosperity ended abruptly in 2008 when the <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">banking system</a> collapsed due to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_property_bubble" title="Irish property bubble">Irish property bubble</a> bursting. Some 25-26% of GDP was needed to bail out failing Irish banks and force banking sector consolidation. This was the largest banking bailout for any country in history, in comparison only 7\xe2\x80\x938% of GDP was needed to bail out failing Finnish banks in its banking crisis in the 1990s. This resulted in a major <a href="/wiki/Post-2008_Irish_economic_downturn" title="Post-2008 Irish economic downturn">financial and political crisis</a> as Ireland entered a <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Emigration rose to 1989 levels as the unemployment rate rose from 4.2% in 2007 to reach 14.6% as of February 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, since 2014, Ireland has seen strong economic growth, dubbed as the "<a href="/wiki/Celtic_Phoenix" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic Phoenix">Celtic Phoenix</a>".\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Northern_Ireland_.281921.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland_(1921\xe2\x80\x93present)">Northern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland (1921\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Northern_Ireland" title="History of Northern Ireland">History of Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<h3><span id=".22A_Protestant_state.22_.281921.E2.80.931972.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;A_Protestant_state&quot;_(1921\xe2\x80\x931972)">"A Protestant state" (1921\xe2\x80\x931972)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: &quot;A Protestant state&quot; (1921\xe2\x80\x931972)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 1920 Government of Ireland Bill created the state of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, which consisted of the six northeastern counties of Londonderry, Tyrone, Fermanagh, Antrim, Down and Armagh.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> From 1921 to 1972, Northern Ireland was governed by a <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Unionist</a> government, based at <a href="/wiki/Stormont,_Belfast" class="mw-redirect" title="Stormont, Belfast">Stormont</a> in east Belfast. Unionist leader and first Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/James_Craig,_1st_Viscount_Craigavon" title="James Craig, 1st Viscount Craigavon">James Craig</a>, declared that it would be "a Protestant State for a Protestant People". Craig\'s goal was to form and preserve Protestant authority in the new state which was above all an effort to secure a unionist majority. In 1926 the majority of the population in the province were Presbyterian and Anglican therefore solidifying Craig\'s Protestant political power. The Ulster Unionist Party thereafter formed every government until 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Discrimination against the minority nationalist community in jobs and housing, and their total exclusion from political power due to the <a href="/wiki/Majoritarian_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Majoritarian system">majoritarian electoral system</a>, led to the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Civil_Rights_Association" title="Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association">Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association</a> in the late 1960s, inspired by Martin Luther King\'s civil rights movement in the United States of America<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>. The military forces of the Northern Protestants and Northern Catholics (IRA) turned to brutal acts of violence to establish power. As time went on it became clear that these two rival states would bring about a civil war. After the Second World War, keeping the cohesion within Stormont seemed impossible; increased economic pressures, solidified Catholic unity, and British involvement ultimately led to Stormont\'s collapse. As the civil rights movement of the United States gained worldwide acknowledgement, Catholics rallied together to achieve a similar socio-political recognition. This resulted in the formation of various organisations such as the Northern Ireland Civil Rights Association (NICRA) in 1967 and the Campaign for Social Justice (CSJ) in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Non-violent protest became an increasingly important factor in mobilising Catholic sympathies and opinion and thus more effective in generating support than actively violent groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army" title="Irish Republican Army">IRA</a>. However, these non-violent protests posed a problem to Northern Ireland\'s prime minister Terrance O\'Neil (1963) because it hampered his efforts to persuade Catholics in Northern Ireland that they too, like their Protestant counterparts, belong within the United Kingdom. Despite O\'Neil\'s reforming efforts there was growing discontent amongst both Catholics and Unionists. In October 1968 a peaceful civil rights march in Derry turned violent as police brutally beat protesters. The outbreak was televised by international media, and as a result the march was highly publicised which further confirmed the socio-political turmoil in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> A violent counter-reaction from conservative unionists led to civil disorder, notably the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bogside" title="Battle of the Bogside">Battle of the Bogside</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_riots_of_August_1969" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Ireland riots of August 1969">Northern Ireland riots of August 1969</a>. To restore order, British troops were deployed to the streets of Northern Ireland at that time.\n</p><p>The violent outbreaks in the late 1960s encouraged and helped strengthen military groups such as the IRA, who posited themselves as the protectors of the working class Catholics who were vulnerable to police and civilian brutality. During the late sixties and early seventies recruitment into the IRA organisation dramatically increased as street and civilian violence worsened. The interjection from the British troops proved to be insufficient to quell the violence and thus solidified the IRA\'s growing military importance.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> On 30 January 1972 the worst tensions came to a head with the events of <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Sunday_(1972)" title="Bloody Sunday (1972)">Bloody Sunday</a>. Paratroops opened fire on civil rights protesters in Derry, killing 13 unarmed civilians. <a href="/wiki/Bloody_Friday_(1972)" title="Bloody Friday (1972)">Bloody Friday</a>, Bloody Sunday, and other violent acts in the early 1970s came to be known as <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">the Troubles</a>. The Stormont parliament was <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#Prorogation" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a> in 1972 and abolished in 1973. Paramilitary private armies such as the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a>, resulted from a split within the IRA, the <a href="/wiki/Official_IRA" class="mw-redirect" title="Official IRA">Official IRA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Liberation_Army" title="Irish National Liberation Army">Irish National Liberation Army</a> fought against the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Regiment" title="Ulster Defence Regiment">Ulster Defence Regiment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Volunteer_Force_(1966)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Volunteer Force (1966)">Ulster Volunteer Force</a>. Moreover, the <a href="/wiki/British_army" class="mw-redirect" title="British army">British army</a> and the (largely Protestant) <a href="/wiki/Royal_Ulster_Constabulary" title="Royal Ulster Constabulary">Royal Ulster Constabulary</a> (RUC) also took part in the chaos that resulted in the deaths of over 3,000 men, women and children, civilians and military. Most of the violence took place in Northern Ireland, but some also spread to England and across the Irish border.\n</p>\n<table style="margin: 0 0 1em 1em; border: 1px solid #8888aa; background: #f7f8ff; clear:right; float:right;" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="padding: 0 5px 0 5px; background:#ccccff; text-align:center;">Irish police href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a></b></small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td><small><a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" class="mw-redirect" title="P\xc3\xb3il\xc3\xadn\xc3\xad Airm">P\xc3\xb3il\xc3\xadn\xc3\xad Airm</a><br />(1922)</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td><small><a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na_Reserve" title="Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na Reserve">Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na Reserve</a><br />(2006)</small>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><br />\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Direct_rule_.281972.E2.80.931999.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_rule_(1972\xe2\x80\x931999)">Direct rule (1972\xe2\x80\x931999)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Direct rule (1972\xe2\x80\x931999)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For the next 27\xc2\xbd years, with the exception of five months in 1974, Northern Ireland was under "<a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_over_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct rule over Northern Ireland">direct rule</a>" with a <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Northern_Ireland" title="Secretary of State for Northern Ireland">Secretary of State for Northern Ireland</a> in the British Cabinet responsible for the departments of the Northern Ireland government. <a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_over_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct rule over Northern Ireland">Direct Rule</a> was designed to be a temporary solution until Northern Ireland was capable of governing itself again. Principal acts were passed by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament of the United Kingdom</a> in the same way as for much of the rest of the UK, but many smaller measures were dealt with by <a href="/wiki/Order_in_Council" title="Order in Council">Order in Council</a> with minimal parliamentary scrutiny.  Attempts were made to establish a power-sharing executive, representing both the nationalist and unionist communities, by the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland_Constitution_Act_1973" title="Northern Ireland Constitution Act 1973">Northern Ireland Constitution Act</a> of 1973 and the <a href="/wiki/Sunningdale_Agreement" title="Sunningdale Agreement">Sunningdale Agreement</a> in December 1973. Both acts however did little to create cohesion between Northern Ireland and the Republic of Ireland. The Constitution Act of 1973 formalised the UK government\'s affirmation of reunification of Ireland by consent only; therefore ultimately delegating the authoritative power of the border question from Stormont to the people of Northern Ireland (and the Republic of Ireland). Conversely, the Sunningdale Agreement included a "provision of a Council of Ireland which held the right to execute executive and harmonizing functions". Most significantly, the Sunningdale Agreement brought together political leaders from Northern Ireland, the Republic of Ireland and the UK to deliberate for the first time since 1925.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The Northern Ireland Constitutional Convention and Jim Prior\'s 1982 assembly were also temporarily implemented; however all failed to either reach consensus or operate in the longer term.\n</p><p>During the 1970s British policy concentrated on defeating the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA) by military means including the policy of <a href="/wiki/Ulsterisation" title="Ulsterisation">Ulsterisation</a> (requiring the RUC and British Army reserve <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Regiment" title="Ulster Defence Regiment">Ulster Defence Regiment</a> to be at the forefront of combating the IRA). Although IRA violence decreased it was obvious that no military victory was on hand in either the short or medium terms. Even Catholics who generally rejected the IRA were unwilling to offer support to a state that seemed to remain mired in <a href="/wiki/Sectarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sectarian">sectarian</a> discrimination, and the Unionists were not interested in Catholic participation in running the state in any case. In the 1980s the IRA attempted to secure a decisive military victory based on massive arms shipments from <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>. When this failed, senior republican figures began to look to broaden the struggle from purely military means. In time this began a move towards <a href="/wiki/TUAS" class="mw-redirect" title="TUAS">military cessation</a>. In 1986 the Irish and British governments signed the <a href="/wiki/Anglo_Irish_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo Irish Agreement">Anglo Irish Agreement</a> signalling a formal partnership in seeking a political solution. The Anglo-Irish Agreement (AIA) recognised the Irish government\'s right to be consulted and heard as well as guaranteed equality of treatment and recognition of the Irish and British identities of the two communities. The agreement also stated that the two governments must implement a cross-border co-operation.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Socially and economically Northern Ireland suffered the worst levels of unemployment in the UK and although high levels of public spending ensured a slow modernisation of public services and moves towards equality, progress was slow in the 1970s and 1980s. Only in the 1990s, when progress toward peace became tangible, did the economic situation brighten. By then the demographics of Northern Ireland had undergone significant change, and more than 40% of the population was Catholic.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Devolution_and_direct_rule_.281999.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Devolution_and_direct_rule_(1999\xe2\x80\x93present)">Devolution and direct rule (1999\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Devolution and direct rule (1999\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>More recently, the <a href="/wiki/Belfast_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Belfast Agreement">Belfast Agreement</a> ("Good Friday Agreement") of 10 April 1998 brought \xe2\x80\x93 on 2 December 1999 \xe2\x80\x93 a degree of power sharing to Northern Ireland, giving both <a href="/wiki/Unionists_(Ireland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Unionists (Ireland)">unionists</a> and nationalists control of limited areas of government. However, both the power-sharing Executive and the elected Assembly were suspended between January and May 2000, and from October 2002 until April 2007, following breakdowns in trust between the political parties involving outstanding issues, including "decommissioning" of paramilitary weapons, <a href="/wiki/Police_Service_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Police Service of Northern Ireland">policing reform</a> and the removal of <a href="/wiki/British_army" class="mw-redirect" title="British army">British army</a> bases. In new elections in 2003, the moderate <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist</a> and (nationalist) <a href="/wiki/Social_Democrat_and_Labour_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Social Democrat and Labour Party">Social Democrat and Labour</a> parties lost their dominant positions to the more hard-line <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Unionist_Party" title="Democratic Unionist Party">Democratic Unionist</a> and (nationalist) <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> parties. On 28 July 2005, the Provisional IRA announced the end of its armed campaign and on 25 September 2005 international weapons inspectors supervised the full disarmament of the PIRA. Eventually, devolution was restored in April 2007.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Ireland">Modern Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Modern Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Ireland\'s economy became more diverse and sophisticated than ever before by integrating itself into the global economy. In 1973, Ireland acceded to the European Economic Community [EEC], precursor to the European Community [EC] and European Union [EU], at the same time as the UK. By the beginning of the 1990s Ireland had transformed itself into a modern industrial economy and generated substantial national income that benefited the entire nation. Although dependence on agriculture still remained high, Ireland\'s industrial economy produced sophisticated goods that rivalled international competition. Ireland\'s international economic boom of the 1990s led to its being called a <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Tiger" title="Celtic Tiger">Celtic Tiger</a>.\n</p><p>The Catholic Church, which once exercised great power, found its influence on socio-political issues in Ireland much reduced. Irish bishops were no longer able to advise and influence the public on how to exercise their political rights. Modern Ireland\'s detachment of the Church from ordinary life can be explained by the increasing disinterest in Church doctrine by younger generations and the questionable morality of the Church\'s representatives. A highly publicised case was that of Eamonn Casey, the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Galway" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Galway">Bishop of Galway</a>, who resigned abruptly in 1992 after it was revealed that he had had an affair with an American woman and had fathered a child. Further controversies and scandals arose concerning paedophile and child-abusing priests. As a result, many in the Irish public began to question the credibility and effectiveness of the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 Ireland closed its embassy at the Vatican, an apparent result of this growing trend.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flags_in_Ireland">Flags in Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Flags in Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Ireland.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Flag_of_Ireland.svg/180px-Flag_of_Ireland.svg.png" width="180" height="90" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Flag_of_Ireland.svg/270px-Flag_of_Ireland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Flag_of_Ireland.svg/360px-Flag_of_Ireland.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Ireland.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Ireland" title="Flag of Ireland">Irish tricolour</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/180px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="180" height="90" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/270px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/360px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">Union Flag</a> (See also <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Flag of Northern Ireland">flag of Northern Ireland</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The national flag of Ireland is a tricolour of green, white and gold. This flag, which bears the colours green for Irish Catholics, orange for Irish Protestants, and white for the desired peace between them, dates to the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The tricolour was first unfurled in public by <a href="/wiki/Young_Ireland" title="Young Ireland">Young Irelander</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Francis_Meagher" title="Thomas Francis Meagher">Thomas Francis Meagher</a> who, using the symbolism of the flag, explained his vision as follows: <i>"The white in the centre signifies a lasting truce between the "Orange" and the "Green," and I trust that beneath its folds the hands of the Irish Protestant and the Irish Catholic may be clasped in generous and heroic brotherhood"</i>. Fellow nationalist <a href="/wiki/John_Mitchel" title="John Mitchel">John Mitchel</a> said of it: "I hope to see that flag one day waving as our national banner."\n</p><p>After its use in the <a href="/wiki/1916_Rising" class="mw-redirect" title="1916 Rising">1916 Rising</a> it became widely accepted by nationalists as the national flag, and was used officially by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republic" title="Irish Republic">Irish Republic</a> (1919\xe2\x80\x9321) and the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> (1922\xe2\x80\x9337).\n</p><p>In 1937 when the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution of Ireland</a> was introduced, the tricolour was formally confirmed as the national flag: "The national flag is the tricolour of green, white and orange." While the tricolour today is the official flag of Ireland, it is not an official flag in Northern Ireland although it is sometimes used unofficially.\n</p><p>The only official flag representing <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> of the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland however its use is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Banner" title="Ulster Banner">Ulster Banner</a> is sometimes used unofficially as a de facto regional flag for Northern Ireland.\n</p><p>Since Partition, there has been no universally-accepted flag to represent the entire island. As a provisional solution for certain sports fixtures, the Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Ireland" title="Provinces of Ireland">Four Provinces</a> enjoys a certain amount of general acceptance and popularity.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>Historically a number of flags have been used, including:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Saint_Patrick%27s_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Patrick&#39;s Flag">Saint Patrick\'s Flag</a> (St Patrick\'s Saltire, St Patrick\'s Cross) which represented Ireland on the <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> after the Act of Union;</li>\n<li>a green flag with a harp (used by most nationalists in the 19th century and which is also the flag of <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a>);</li>\n<li>a blue flag with a harp used from the 18th century onwards by many nationalists (now the standard of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Ireland" title="President of Ireland">President of Ireland</a>);</li>\n<li>the <a href="/wiki/Irish_tricolour" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish tricolour">Irish tricolour</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>St Patrick\'s Saltire was formerly used to represent the island of Ireland by the all-island <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rugby_Football_Union" title="Irish Rugby Football Union">Irish Rugby Football Union</a> (IRFU), before adoption of the four-provinces flag. The <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_Athletic_Association" title="Gaelic Athletic Association">Gaelic Athletic Association</a> (GAA) uses the tricolour to represent the whole island.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_historians" title="List of Irish historians">List of Irish historians</a></div>\n<p>Ireland has a very large historiography, contributed by scholars in Ireland, North America, and Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  There has been both a standard interpretation and, since the late 1930s, a good deal of revisionism.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  One of the most important themes has always been Irish nationalism\xe2\x80\x94what Alfred Markey, calls:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>the received nationalist tale replete with heroes, villains and a host of stock elements, has a long history and has exercised a particularly important influence on the development of Irish identity.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Nationalism has led to numerous monographs and debates.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A great deal of attention has focused on the <a href="/wiki/Irish_revolutionary_period" title="Irish revolutionary period">Irish revolutionary period</a>, 1912-23. Starting in 2012 a series of conferences on "Reflecting on a decade of War and Revolution in Ireland 1912-1923: Historians and Public History" brought together hundreds of academics, teachers, and the general public.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Britain">Relations with Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Relations with Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Ireland in some ways was the first acquisition of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Marshall says historians continue to debate whether Ireland should be considered part of the British Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>  Recent work by historians pays special attention to continuing Imperial aspects of Irish history,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Atlantic Ocean history,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and the role of migration in forming the Irish diaspora across the Empire and North America.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_approaches">Recent approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Ireland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Recent approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As historiography evolves, new approaches have been applied to the Irish situation. Studies of women, and gender relationships more generally, had been rare before 1990; they now are commonplace with over 3000 books and articles.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Postcolonialism" title="Postcolonialism">Postcolonialism</a> is an approach in several academic disciplines that seeks to analyze, explain, and respond to the cultural legacies of colonialism and imperialism. The emphasis is usually on the human consequences of controlling a country and establishing settlers for the economic exploitation of the native people and their land.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a of Irish history">Timeline of Irish history</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a "Indarba n-gennti a h-Ere, .\xc3\xad. longport Atha Cliath o Mael Findia m.
